Warning Against Disrespecting Prophets
Sanusi guards against neglect of prophets' rank and criticizes Jews and historians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grave sin of disrespecting prophets based on their human appearance and the importance of upholding their lofty rank as taught by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Disrespecting prophets due to their human appearance is heedlessness and a grave error.

Sanusi continues to clarify the attributes of prophethood by guarding against a common pitfall: neglecting the prophets' exalted status because they appear as humans like us. He warns that even great scholars can fall into error if they rely on superficial readings of scripture. In this passage, he condemns those who have disrespected the prophets and promises to provide examples later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning and the force of Sanusi's warning.
Notice the emotional intensity in his condemnation of the Jews.
2
Identify the two groups criticized
Sanusi criticizes the Jews for their disrespect, and also ignorant historians and exegetes. Understand why each group's error is dangerous and how they justify it.
The ignorant rely on apparent meanings of Scripture without proper knowledge.
3
Reflect on the lesson's core warning
Ask yourself: Why is it so important to maintain respect for prophets despite their human qualities? How can one avoid the error of the Jews?
Sanusi promises to give examples later, highlighting the seriousness of the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولنا : ( التي لا تنافي عُلُو رتبتهم ) فاحترزنا بهِ مِنَ الغفلة عن جنابهم الرفيع ، والتفريط بسبب مشاهدة ظواهرهم البشريَّةِ ؛ في مراعاة قدرِهم العليّ، وملاحظةِ اعتناء المولى العظيم بهم ، ورفع مَقامِهِمُ الأكمل فوق جميع الخلق . وقد ضَلَّتِ اليهودُ - أدامَ اللهُ ذِلَتَهم - فأساؤوا الأدب ، ووصفوا أنبياء الله تعالى ورُسُلَهُ عليهم الصلاة والسلامُ بمَساوِيَ لا يليقُ أَنْ يُوصَفَ بها مَنْ هو أدنى منهم في غايةٍ ، وربَّما أدخلَ بعضُ جهلة المُؤرّخينَ والمُفسّرينَ بعضَ ذلك في كتبهم ، وافتتنوا بذلك ، وفتنوا به مَنْ يُطالعُهُ مِنَ الجهلة ! فنسأل الله تعالى العافية مِنْ زلاتِ مَنْ يُقتدى بهِ ؛ فَإِنَّهُ يَضِلُّ بسببٍ زلَّتِهِ وفتنته عالَمٌ كثيرٌ ، ولا حول ولا قُوَّةَ إِلا بِاللهِ ، وربَّما يَعْتَرُّونَ بذلك - لقِلَّةِ تحصيلهم ، وعدم تحقيقهم - بظواهر مِنَ الكتابِ والسنَّةِ ، سنشير إنْ شاءَ الله بعد هذا إلى جُملةٍ منها ؛ ليُعرَفَ منها غيرها
As for our statement: 'which does not contradict their lofty rank,' we guarded by it against heedlessness of their exalted status and against neglect, due to observing their human appearances, in observing their high standing and noticing the care of the Mighty Lord for them and raising their perfect station above all creation. And the Jews — may God perpetuate their humiliation — went astray, so they behaved disrespectfully and described the prophets of Allah and His messengers — peace and blessings be upon them — with faults that do not befit one who is lower than them in any degree. And perhaps some ignorant historians and exegetes inserted some of that into their books, and were tempted by that, and tempted others who read it among the ignorant. So we ask Allah the Exalted for safety from the slips of those who are followed; for indeed, due to the slip and temptation of one, a great world goes astray, and there is no power or strength except with Allah. And perhaps they resort to that — due to their lack of attainment and lack of verification — by [citing] apparent meanings from the Book and the Sunnah, which we will, Allah willing, point to a number of them after this, so that others may be known from them.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage begins with an explanation of a previous statement (from an earlier part not in this window). Sanusi explains that he mentioned 'which does not contradict their lofty rank' to guard against two extremes: underestimating prophets because of their human appearances, and overestimating them (or rather, not respecting them properly). He then condemns the Jews and certain historians/exegetes who attribute faults to prophets, warning that such errors arise from relying on apparent meanings of texts without proper knowledge. He promises to give examples later.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is acceptable to criticize prophets based on their human actions or apparent mistakes.
Correction: Sanusi shows that this is a grave error; prophets' lofty rank must be respected above all, and the Jews were condemned for such disrespect.

Checkpoint

A) Ignoring their human qualities
B) Overlooking their high rank due to their human appearance
C) Following the Jews
D) Using the Book and Sunnah

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Obligation of Ta'wil for Texts Implying Deficiency
Sanusi states the rule for allegorical interpretation of texts implying deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that any scriptural text implying deficiency in prophets or angels must be interpreted allegorically (ta'wil), and why this is necessary.

Core Idea
Scripture's apparent flaws about prophets must be read through ta'wil.

Sanusi now establishes a crucial hermeneutical principle for protecting the honor of prophets and angels. When the Quran or Sunnah contains a phrase that seems to attribute sin or imperfection to them, we cannot take it literally. Instead, we must perform ta'wil, an allegorical interpretation that upholds their infallibility. This short but powerful rule guides how we approach many ambiguous texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic heading and the explanatory sentence. Notice that the passage is a clear ruling: any text implying deficiency must be interpreted.
Pay attention to the phrase 'أوهم نقصاً' which means 'gives the impression of deficiency'.
2
Understand the scope
The rule applies to both prophets and angels, and to both the Quran and the Sunnah. No exception is made for any text.
Reflect on why angels are included: they are also infallible in Islamic theology.
3
Reflect on the necessity of ta'wil
Ask yourself: why can't we take such texts literally? The answer lies in the doctrine of 'isma (infallibility) of prophets and angels. Ta'wil preserves their perfection.
This connects to the broader principle that Allah and His messengers are free from deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ كلُّ ما أوهم نقصاً في حقٌّ الأنبياء والملائكة عليهم السلام يجب تأويله ]
وبهذا تعرفُ أنَّ كلَّ ما أوهم في حقهم أو حق الملائكة نقصاً مِنَ الكتاب أو السنَّةِ .. وجب تأويله
[Everything that gives the impression of deficiency in the status of the prophets and angels, peace be upon them, must be interpreted (taʾwīl).]
And by this you know that every apparent meaning in the Book or the Sunnah that gives the impression of deficiency regarding them or regarding the angels must be interpreted.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage establishes an important rule in Islamic theology: any scriptural text that seems to imply a deficiency (naqṣ) in the prophets or angels must be interpreted metaphorically (taʾwīl). The heading states: 'Everything that gives the impression of deficiency in the status of the prophets and angels must be interpreted.' Then the author explains: 'And by this you know that every apparent meaning in the Book or the Sunnah that gives the impression of deficiency regarding them or regarding the angels must be interpreted.' This is a foundational principle to protect the honor of prophets and angels, as they are infallible (maʿṣūm). Apparent meanings that seem to attribute sin or imperfection to them are not to be taken literally but must be interpreted in a way that preserves their perfection. This rule is part of the broader methodology of dealing with ambiguous texts (mutashābihāt) in the Quran and Sunnah.

Key Terms
أوهم نقصا (gives impression of deficiency)
تأويل (allegorical interpretation)
الأنبياء والملائكة (prophets and angels)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any apparent meaning in scripture must be taken literally.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that texts implying deficiency in prophets or angels require ta'wil, not literal acceptance.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what must be done with a Quranic verse that appears to attribute a deficiency to a prophet?
A) Accept it literally.
B) Reject the verse.
C) Interpret it allegorically (ta'wil).
D) Ignore it.

Key Takeaway
Any apparent meaning in the Quran or Sunnah that suggests deficiency in prophets or angels must be interpreted allegorically to preserve their perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a story about a prophet in the Quran that seems to show them making a mistake, what is the first thought that crosses your mind? Do you immediately trust that there is a correct interpretation that preserves their honor, or do you feel doubt? This lesson gives you a clear rule: ta'wil is obligatory. Will you commit to seeking the proper interpretation whenever you encounter such texts, rather than relying on your initial impression?
?
Have you ever heard someone criticize a prophet based on a literal reading of scripture? How did you respond? Did you defend their honor with knowledge, or did you remain silent? From now on, you have the tool of ta'wil—will you use it to protect the status of Allah's chosen ones, even if it means challenging popular misunderstandings?
?
This principle of ta'wil requires you to submit your intellect to the scholars' consensus that prophets and angels are perfect. Is there any part of you that resists this, preferring a more 'literal' reading that seems more 'honest'? Examine your heart: are you willing to trust the interpretive tradition over your own unaided reason when it comes to the honor of those whom Allah has honored?

Textual Evidence for Prophetic Sin and Repentance
Author compiles Quranic verses and hadiths reporting prophetic mistakes and repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural evidence that Sunni scholars cite to support the view that prophets can commit minor sins and seek forgiveness, and how these texts are interpreted within the doctrine of prophetic sinlessness.

Core Idea
Prophets sought forgiveness, but their errors were minor and never major sins.

This lesson presents a collection of Quranic verses and prophetic hadiths that describe prophets—including Adam, Jonah, David, Joseph, Moses, and Muhammad—seeking forgiveness or being rebuked. These texts are central to the theological discussion on prophetic sinlessness (ʿisma). As we read them, consider how scholars reconcile these apparent lapses with the belief that prophets are preserved from major sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author is compiling evidence without commentary.
2
Identify the prophets mentioned
List the prophets referenced: Adam, Jonah, David, Joseph, Moses, Abraham, Solomon, and Muhammad. Note the specific actions or statements attributed to each.
3
Categorize the type of evidence
Group the citations into Quranic verses (direct speech about prophets) and hadiths (prophetic statements). Observe how the hadiths focus on the Prophet Muhammad's own istighfar.
4
Ponder the theological implications
Ask yourself: If prophets are sinless, why do these texts show them asking forgiveness? How might scholars argue that these are minor errors or permissible actions?
This connects to the learning objective.

-
ومِنْ ذلك أيضاً : ما قصَّ مِنْ قَصص الأنبياء ـ غير سيدنا ومولانا محمَّدٍ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ وعليهم أجمعين - كقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَعَصَى عادَمُ رَبَّهُ فغوى له [ طه : ۱۲۱] .

بِهَا:

And the saying of the Exalted, Great in speech: 'He frowned and turned away because the blind man came to him' [Quran 80:1-2].

And also from that: what He narrated from the stories of the prophets — other than our master and lord Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and upon them all — such as His saying the Exalted: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and erred' [Quran 20:121].
And His saying the Exalted: 'Then when He gave them a righteous child, they ascribed partners to Him in what He had given them...' [Quran 7:190] the verse.
And His saying the Exalted, also reporting from Adam, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves...' [Quran 7:23] the verse.
:
And His saying, Glorified be He, about Jonah, peace be upon him: 'Glory be to You, indeed I was of the wrongdoers' [Quran 21:87] and what He mentioned of his story and the story of David, peace be upon him, and His saying about him: 'So he asked forgiveness of his Lord and fell down bowing and repented... up to His saying: 'Oft-returning' [Quran 38:24-25].
And the story of our Prophet and lord Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, with Zayd his freedman and Zaynab, and His saying the Exalted: 'And you concealed within yourself what Allah would bring to light, and you feared the people while Allah has more right that you fear Him' [Quran 33:37].
And His saying the Exalted about Joseph, peace be upon him: 'And certainly she desired him, and he would have desired her...' [Quran 12:24], and what He narrated of his story with his brothers.
And His saying the Exalted about Moses, peace be upon him: 'So Moses struck him with his fist and killed him. He said: This is from the work of Satan' [Quran 28:15].

The saying of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, in his supplication: 'Forgive me what I have done before and what I will do later, and what I have concealed and what I have revealed' (1) and similar, and the mention of the prophets, peace and blessings be upon them, in the standing place of their sins when intercession is asked of them, and his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Indeed, a veil comes over my heart, so I ask forgiveness of Allah' (2).
And in the hadith of Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him: 'Indeed, I ask forgiveness of Allah and repent to Him more than seventy times a day' (3).
And His saying the Exalted: 'And unless You forgive me and have mercy upon me, I will be among the losers' [Quran 11:47], and He, Blessed and Exalted, had said to him: 'And do not plead with Me for those who have wronged; indeed, they are to be drowned' [Quran 11:37]. And He said the Exalted about Abraham: 'And who I aspire that He will forgive me my sin on the Day of Recompense' [Quran 26:82].
And His saying the Exalted about Moses: 'I have repented to You' [Quran 7:143].
And His saying, Great and Exalted: 'And certainly We tried Solomon' [Quran 38:34].
And His saying, Great and Exalted: 'Then when the night covered him, he saw a star and said: This is my Lord' [Quran 6:76].
(1) Narrated by al-Bukhari (1120, 7385) from our master Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, and Muslim (771) from our master Ali, may Allah be pleased with him.
(2) Narrated by Muslim (2702) from the hadith of our master al-Agharr al-Muzani, may Allah be pleased with him.
(3) Narrated by al-Bukhari (6307).
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a collection of textual proofs (adilla naqliyya) gathered by the author to support a theological discussion on prophetic sinlessness (ʿisma). The author lists Quranic verses and prophetic hadiths that appear to show that prophets—including Adam, Yunus, Dawood, Yusuf, Musa, Ibrahim, and Muhammad—committed acts for which they sought forgiveness or were rebuked. The verses are cited without explanatory comment, so the passage functions as a dossier of evidence. The inclusion of the hadith about the Prophet Muhammad's frequent istighfar and his statement of a 'veil over his heart' further suggests that even the best of creation experienced moments that required divine pardon. The author likely intends this collection to be used as proof that prophets can commit minor sins (ṣaghāʾir) or errors (khaṭaʾ) before or after prophethood, while maintaining that they are protected from major sins (kabāʾir). However, within the text itself, no conclusion is drawn; the passage simply presents the raw material for the argument.

Key Terms
عصمة الأنبياء
صغائر
استغفار
كبرى
خطأ الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage proves that prophets committed major sins (kabāʾir).
Correction: Sunni scholars interpret these texts as referring to minor sins (ṣaghāʾir) or errors before prophethood, not major sins. Prophets are protected from major sins.

Checkpoint
Which prophet is quoted as saying, 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves'?
A) Muhammad
B) Adam
C) Yunus
D) Musa

Key Takeaway
The collected verses and hadiths show that prophets sought forgiveness for minor errors, but these are not considered major sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) was rebuked for frowning at the blind man, do you examine your own treatment of those you consider 'unimportant'? What specific change will you make today in how you treat someone in need?
?
The prophets constantly sought forgiveness—even for what seems minor. How often do you seek forgiveness for your own shortcomings, especially those you justify as 'not a big deal'? Will you commit to saying Astaghfirullah at least 70 times today, following the Prophet's example?
?
If the best of creation admitted his need for Allah's forgiveness, how can you afford to be proud or neglectful of your own sins? What is one sin you have been ignoring that you will sincerely repent of right now?

The Two Aspects of the Prophet's 'Sin'
The author explains two reasons for ascribing sin to the Prophet in the context of his infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons why the Prophet is described as having sin attributed to him despite his infallibility.

Core Idea
The Prophet's attributed sin highlights divine favor and his exclusive standard of perfection.

In the Quran, Allah says to the Prophet, 'That Allah may forgive you your sin.' How can the infallible Prophet be associated with sin? The author explains that this attribution is not literal. He gives two aspects that preserve the Prophet's perfection while acknowledging his unique rank. This passage unpacks the wisdom behind the wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the author's phrase 'and the point of turning away from defining sin with the definite article to defining it with the addition to him'. This sets up the two aspects.
The passage begins with a brief introduction before the two aspects.
2
Identify the two aspects
The author explicitly lists two aspects (وجهان). Find them and understand each one. The first is about establishing favor, the second about alerting by the subtle to the obvious.
The second aspect includes examples like familiarity with obedience and desiring Paradise.
3
Reflect on how each aspect preserves infallibility
Consider why each explanation keeps the Prophet sinless. The first shows that the 'sin' is only a possibility; the second shows it is a sin relative to his high station, not an actual moral failure.
This connects to the broader doctrine of prophetic infallibility (عصمة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والهممِ القدسية العالية .. ما استأصل شأفةَ كلُّ ذنبٍ ، وستر بسببه المولى الكريم عنه سوابق كل عيب ولواحقه . ونكتةُ العدول عن تعريف الذنبِ بالألف واللام إلى تعريفه بالإضافة إليه عليه الصلاة والسلام وجهان : أحدهما : تقرير النعمةِ عليهِ ؛ بأنَّ هذا الذنب الذي عُصِمَ منه هو ذنب له بحسَبِ الإمكانِ العقلي والقبولِ البشري العادي ، وفي العصمة مِنْ ذلكَ معَ القَبولِ مِنَ المِنَّةِ عليهِ والفضل العظيم ما لا يخفى الثاني : يحتمل أن تكونَ الإضافة للتنبيه بالخفي على الجلي وبالأدنى على الأعلى ؛ أي : سترنا عنكَ الذنب الذي يُتوهّم وصوله إليكَ ، ويُعَدُّ ذنباً بالنسبة إليكَ ، وإنْ كانَ حسنةً بالنسبة إلى غيرِكَ ؛ كالأُنس مثلاً بالطاعة ، والقصدِ بفعلها نيل ما يلائم النفسَ في الجِنَانِ مِنَ المشتهيات ، ونحو ذلكَ ممَّا هو كثير لائقٌ بِمَقامِ أَهل الحجابِ مِنَ الزهّادِ والمتعبدينَ ، وإذا سُتِرَ عنه هذا النوع ، واستُؤصِلَتْ سوابقه ولواحقُهُ ، وإنْ كانَ ليس ذنباً حقيقياً ، بل هو كمال في حق العموم ... فأخرى سائر الذنوب التي هي ذنوب حقيقية في حق العام والخاص ؛ كالزنا وشرب الخمر والغيبة ونحوها
And the high holy aspirations... what uproots the root of every sin, and by which the Generous Lord conceals from him the precedents and consequences of every defect. And the point of turning away from defining sin with the definite article to defining it with the addition to him, peace and blessings be upon him, is two aspects: One of them: establishing the favor upon him; that this sin from which he was protected is a sin relative to him according to rational possibility and ordinary human acceptance, and in the protection from that along with the acceptance, there is from the favor upon him and the great bounty what is not hidden. The second: It is possible that the addition is for alerting by the subtle to the obvious and by the lower to the higher; i.e., 'We concealed from you the sin that is imagined to reach you, and it is considered a sin relative to you, even if it is a good deed relative to others; like, for example, familiarity with obedience, and intending by performing it to attain what suits the soul in the Gardens of desires, and the like of that which is abundant and befitting the station of the people of the veil among the ascetics and worshippers. And when this type is concealed from him, and its precedents and consequences are uprooted, even if it is not a real sin, but rather a perfection for the general public... then what about the rest of the sins that are real sins for the general and specific? like adultery, drinking wine, backbiting, and the like.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why the phrase 'your sin' is used for the Prophet despite his infallibility. The author gives two aspects (وجوهان). First, the attribution is a gift (تقرير النعمة): the sin from which he is protected is only a sin in terms of rational possibility and ordinary human capacity, but he is protected from it, which is a great favor. Second, it is a subtle reminder: the sin is only a sin relative to his high station, while for others it would be a good deed. The author gives examples like familiarity with obedience or desiring pleasures of Paradise. If such minor things are concealed from the Prophet, then real sins like adultery and drinking are even more impossible for him.

Key Terms
ذنب
عصمة
إضافة
نكتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet committed actual sins.
Correction: The attribution is relative and rhetorical; it either highlights divine favor or serves as a subtle reminder of his perfection, not indicating real moral failure.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the phrase 'your sin' is used for the Prophet?
A) To indicate the Prophet committed actual sins
B) To establish favor on him or to alert by subtle to obvious
C) To show that the Prophet is like ordinary humans
D) To allow the Prophet to feel guilt

Key Takeaway
The attribution of sin to the Prophet is a rhetorical device that both honors him and affirms the perfection of his infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that the Prophet was protected even from the 'sin' of desiring reward in Paradise. How often do you examine your own intentions in worship? Are you willing to ask Allah to purify your motives even when it costs you the comfort of expectation?
?
If the Prophet's attributed sin is actually a sign of divine favor, how does that reshape your understanding of Allah's treatment of you? Can you see your own shortcomings as opportunities for grace rather than mere failures?
?
This lesson reveals that even subtle imperfections are 'sins' relative to the Prophet's rank. What would it mean for you to hold yourself to a standard where your small, overlooked faults become serious matters? Are you ready to confront them today?

Why Was the Prophet Commanded to Seek Forgiveness?
Interpreting the command for the Prophet to seek forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of the verse 'Ask forgiveness for your sin' and why the author prefers the interpretation of pure worship.

Core Idea
The Prophet's command to seek forgiveness was either for his nation or for his own increase in rank.

How could the infallible Prophet be commanded to seek forgiveness? This verse has puzzled many. Imam Sanusi presents two scholarly interpretations: one directs the command to the ummah, and the other sees it as an act of pure worship that elevates the Prophet's rank and reminds of divine favor. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two interpretations presented. Pay attention to the author's preference.
The passage is concise; each word carries theological weight.
2
Identify the two interpretations
List the two possible meanings of the command: 1) addressed to the Prophet but meant for the ummah, 2) a genuine command to the Prophet as pure worship.
The second interpretation is further supported by reasons: increase in rank, reminder of infallibility, and indication of pure favor.
3
Understand the key term 'ta'abbud mahd' (تعبد محض)
This means an act of worship whose wisdom may not be fully grasped but is performed out of obedience. Here, the command is not because of a sin but to elevate the Prophet.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some might think the Prophet had sins. This passage clarifies that the command is either for the ummah or for the Prophet's honor, not due to a sin.
The author links this to the verse in Surah al-Fath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ﴿وَاسْتَغْفِرْ لِذَنْبِكَ ﴾ [محمد : ١٩] : فقيل : إِنَّهُ خطاب له والمرادُ أُمَّتُه ، ويحتمل أن يكونَ أُمر بذلك على سبيل التعبدِ المحضِ ؛ زيادةً في رفع الدرجاتِ ، وتذكيراً لنعمة العصمة بطلب دوامها ، وإشارةً إلى أنّها محض فضل بلا وجوب ولا استحقاق . ونكتة إضافةِ الذنب إليه هنا : ما سبق في آية سورة ( الفتح ) ، وهذا الوجه أقربُ ، والله تعالى أعلم .
As for His statement, the Exalted: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin' [Muhammad 19]: It is said: it is an address to him but the intended is his nation, and it is possible that he was commanded to do that on the way of pure worship; as an increase in raising ranks, and as a reminder of the blessing of infallibility by asking for its continuance, and an indication that it is pure favor without obligation or merit. And the point of adding the sin to him here is what preceded in the verse of Surah al-Fath. And this aspect is closer, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of the verse commanding the Prophet to seek forgiveness. The author presents two views. First, the command is actually for the ummah, using the Prophet as addressee. Second, it is a command to the Prophet as pure worship (تعبد محض) to increase his ranks and to remind him that his infallibility is a pure gift. The author prefers the second view, linking it to the earlier explanation about the addition of sin.

Key Terms
استغفار
تعبد محض
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet (peace be upon him) had sins that needed forgiveness.
Correction: The command to seek forgiveness is either for the ummah or for the Prophet's own elevation and recognition of divine favor, not due to sin.

Checkpoint

A) It is a command to the ummah
B) It is a command to the Prophet for pure worship and increase in rank
C) It means the Prophet committed a sin
D) It is abrogated

Key Takeaway
The command for the Prophet to seek forgiveness is understood either as directed to the ummah or as a pure act of worship to elevate his rank and acknowledge divine favor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you ever catch yourself treating them as a way to earn something from Allah, rather than as a pure act of submission? How can you transform your intention to match the Prophet's example of seeking forgiveness purely for increase in rank and acknowledgment of favor?
?
The Prophet, who is sinless, was commanded to seek forgiveness. Does this expose your own negligence in seeking repentance? What would it look like for you to sincerely seek forgiveness today, not just for sins but as a reminder of your absolute dependence on Allah's grace?
?
If even the Prophet's high station is 'pure favor without obligation or merit,' what does that say about your own deeds? Are you relying on your good works or on Allah's mercy? Write down one concrete change you can make to shift from a sense of entitlement to humble gratitude.

The True Meaning of 'Removing the Burden'
Sanusi interprets Quran 94:2 in light of prophetic sinlessness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of Quran 94:2 in light of the Prophet's sinlessness.

Core Idea
The Prophet's burden refers not to sin but to protection from sin.

In this brief passage, Sanusi addresses a common misinterpretation of Quran 94:2. He explains that when the verse speaks of removing the Prophet's burden, it must be understood in the context of his sinlessness (isma). This lesson clarifies the true meaning: divine protection, not forgiveness of actual sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the passage
Read the Arabic verse and the author's commentary. Notice how the author begins with 'if burden is taken as sin'.
The phrase 'الوزر' can mean burden or sin.
2
Understand the two interpretations
The author presents two possible interpretations: burden as sin vs. removal as protection. Focus on the conditional 'if taken as sin'.
The author favors the interpretation that removal means protection.
3
Analyze the author's reasoning
The author says removal means 'protection from it and from its precedents and consequences.' This emphasizes that the Prophet never committed sin nor was affected by its causes or results.
This protects the doctrine of isma (sinlessness).
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this interpretation matters for the belief in prophethood. It upholds the Prophet's complete innocence and divine protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَوَضَعْنَا عَنكَ وِزْرَكَ ﴾ [الشرح : ٢] : ففيه أقوال كثيرة ، والأظهرُ - إِنْ حُمِلَ الوزر على الذنب - : أَنَّ وضعَهُ حينئذ بمعنى الحفظ منهُ ومِنْ سوابقه ولواحقه ؛ حتى لا يحمل مؤنةً مِنْ مونه
وإضافة الوزرِ إليه : نكتتُه أيضاً ما سبق .
As for His statement, the Exalted: 'And We removed from you your burden' [Sharh 2]: There are many sayings about it, and the most apparent - if 'burden' is taken as sin - is that its removal then means protection from it and from its precedents and consequences; so that he does not carry the burden of it. And the adding of the burden to him: its point is also what preceded.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage interprets the verse 'We removed from you your burden'. The author states that if 'burden' is interpreted as sin (which is the context), then 'removing' means protecting him from that sin and its consequences, so he does not bear any burden. The addition of 'your burden' follows the same earlier point about attributing sin to the Prophet.

Key Terms
وزر
وضع
حفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet Muhammad had a burden of sin that was removed through forgiveness.
Correction: The verse means protection from sin and its consequences, not forgiveness of actual sin.

Checkpoint

A) The Prophet committed sin and it was forgiven
B) Protection from sin and its consequences
C) The burden was transferred to others
D) The verse is metaphorical with no relation to sin

Key Takeaway
The removal of the Prophet's burden in Quran 94:2 signifies divine protection from sin, not the forgiveness of sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet was protected from sin so that his example would be flawless. Do you truly accept that his every action is the perfect guidance, or do you secretly excuse some of your own shortcomings by thinking he was just a fallible human? What would change if you fully internalized his sinlessness?
?
When you read 'We removed your burden,' does your heart feel relieved that the Prophet was spared from sin, or does it overlook the verse's deeper meaning? How can you train yourself to see divine protection rather than divine punishment in this verse?
?
If you were accused of something false, how would you want others to understand the truth about you? How much more does the Prophet deserve your defense of his honor? What step can you take this week to defend his character when you hear a suggestion of flaw?

Divine Forgiveness as Honor
The author explains that the phrase is honorific, not a rebuke.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'May Allah forgive you' addressed to the Prophet is an honorific, not a rebuke.

Core Idea
Divine forgiveness can be an honor, not a correction.

This lesson examines a Quranic verse that might seem like a rebuke but is actually a form of honor. The author clarifies that the phrase is an expression of respect and magnification, similar to customary greetings for dignitaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and author's statement
Read the Arabic quotation from Surah Tawbah and the author's explanation. Notice the direct claim: 'There is no blame in it at all.'
The verse is often misunderstood.
2
Identify the contrast
The author juxtaposes 'no blame' with 'honor and magnification'. Recognize that forgiveness here is not a reprimand but a token of respect.
3
Understand the analogy
The author gives an analogy: just as we say 'May Allah set you right' to great people as a greeting, so is 'May Allah forgive you' an honorific phrase.
This shows cultural context.
4
Apply the concept
Reflect on how this interpretation protects the Prophet's status. Avoid the common misunderstanding that the Prophet was rebuked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا قولُهُ تعالى : عَفَا اللهُ عَنكَ ﴾ [التوبة : ٤٣] : فلا معاتبة فيهِ بوجه ، بل فيهِ تَكرِمةٌ وتعظيم ، كما يُقالُ في استفتاح الكلامِ معَ العظماء : أصلحَكَ اللهُ وأَعزَّكَ (۱)
As for His statement, the Exalted: 'May Allah forgive you' [Tawbah 43]: There is no blame in it at all, but rather in it is honor and magnification, as it is said at the beginning of speech with great people: 'May Allah set you right and strengthen you.'
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage interprets the verse 'Afā Allahu 'anka' (May Allah forgive you). The author denies that it implies any rebuke; rather, it is an expression of honoring and magnifying the Prophet, similar to saying 'May Allah set you right and strengthen you' when addressing great people.

Key Terms
عفو
تكريم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet was rebuked by Allah in this verse.
Correction: The verse is actually an expression of honor and magnification, not a rebuke.

Checkpoint
How does the author interpret the phrase 'May Allah forgive you' in the Quran?
A) As a rebuke for a mistake
B) As a form of honoring and magnifying
C) As a direct command to seek forgiveness
D) As a metaphorical statement with no meaning

Key Takeaway
The Quranic phrase 'May Allah forgive you' is an expression of honor and magnification, not a rebuke.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Do you only ask for forgiveness when you feel guilty, or can you begin to ask as an act of drawing closer to Allah, even without a specific sin?
?
Think of a person you respect deeply. If they said 'please forgive me' for something trivial, would you interpret it as weakness or as a gesture of honor toward your relationship? How can you train yourself to see the request for forgiveness as an opportunity to elevate the other person, just as this passage teaches?

The Beginning of Interpreting 'Were It Not for a Previous Decree'
Author begins interpreting Quran 8:68, linking to divine decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author begins to interpret Quran 8:68, highlighting that a divine decree prevented punishment for the companions at Badr.

Core Idea
A prior decree from Allah averted punishment in the Badr spoils incident.

This lesson presents the opening of the author's interpretation of Qur'an 8:68, a verse often discussed regarding prophetic infallibility. The passage is incomplete, but it clearly establishes that the apparent meaning involves a previous decree from Allah that prevented a great punishment. We will examine the verse, the author's initial comment, and note the context of the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic verse and its literal translation. Notice the conditional phrase 'lawla' (were it not for), which sets up the core idea.
The verse is about the spoils taken by the companions after Badr.
2
Identify the author's interpretive approach
The author states 'the most apparent' meaning, indicating a choice among possible interpretations. Focus on what he presents as the clear sense: a prior decree from Allah.
The text cuts off after 'that preceded...', so we only have the beginning of his explanation.
3
Understand the context of the decree
Reflect on why a divine decree would be needed to prevent punishment. This connects to debates about whether the Prophet or the companions were at fault. The author implies that without the decree, punishment would have occurred.
The full discussion likely clarifies that the decree shows divine mercy and prophetic protection.
4
Recognize the incomplete nature of the passage
Note that the passage ends mid-sentence. This means we must await further explanation. Use this as a reminder that scholarly texts often require patience and subsequent pages.
The lesson ends here, but the interpretation continues on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ( لَوْلَا كِتَبٌ مِّنَ اللَّهِ سَبَقَ لَمَسَّكُمْ فِيمَا أَخَذْتُمْ عَذَابُ عظيم [الأنفال : ٦٨] : فالأظهرُ أنَّ معناه : لولا كتابٌ مِنَ اللهِ سبقَ
As for His statement, the Exalted: 'Were it not for a previous decree from Allah, a great punishment would have touched you for what you took' [Anfal 68]: The most apparent is that its meaning is: Were it not for a previous decree from Allah that preceded...

Commentary
This passage begins the interpretation of the verse regarding the Battle of Badr. The author states the most apparent meaning: 'were it not for a previous decree from Allah that preceded...' but the text cuts off. The verse is often discussed in the context of prophetic infallibility, and the author likely continues to explain that the 'punishment' is not for the Prophet but for the companions, or that the decree prevented punishment. However, since the passage is incomplete within this window, we note that.

The Prophet's Command to Seek Forgiveness: Not a Sin
Sanusi explains the verse 'Ask forgiveness for your sin' and defends prophetic infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two interpretations of the command to the Prophet to seek forgiveness, and how they preserve his infallibility.

Core Idea
The command to seek forgiveness does not imply sin; it is either for the community or a devotional act.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a potentially troubling verse: God commands the Prophet to ask forgiveness for his sin. How can a sinless prophet have a sin? Sanusi presents two interpretations that preserve the doctrine of infallibility while explaining the verse's deeper wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the two interpretations Sanusi offers.
Pay attention to the phrase 'increase in rank'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find and note the two possible explanations: one involving the community, the other a devotional act. Understand the reasoning behind each.
The second interpretation includes multiple subtleties.
3
Understand the preferred view
Sanusi indicates the second interpretation is closer. Reflect on why it better preserves prophetic infallibility and what it teaches about grace.
Consider the connection to Surah Al-Fath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ﴿وَاسْتَغْفِرْ لِذَنْبِكَ ﴾ [محمد : ١٩] : فقيل : إِنَّهُ خطاب له والمرادُ أُمَّتُه ، ويحتمل أن يكونَ أُمر بذلك على سبيل التعبدِ المحضِ ؛ زيادةً في رفع الدرجاتِ ، وتذكيراً لنعمة العصمة بطلب دوامها ، وإشارةً إلى أنّها محض فضل بلا وجوب ولا استحقاق . ونكتة إضافةِ الذنب إليه هنا : ما سبق في آية سورة ( الفتح ) ، وهذا الوجه أقربُ ، والله تعالى أعلم .
And as for His saying, the Exalted: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin' [Muhammad 47:19]: It was said: It is an address to him, but the intent is his community. And it is possible that he was commanded to do that purely as an act of worship, to increase rank, and to remind of the blessing of infallibility (al-‘iṣma) by requesting its continuation, and as an indication that it is pure grace without obligation or merit. And the subtlety of attributing sin to him here is what preceded in the verse of Surah Al-Fath. And this view is closer, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a potentially problematic verse (Surah Muhammad 47:19) where the Prophet is commanded to ask forgiveness for his sin. The author provides two interpretations: (1) it is addressed to the Prophet but meant for his community, so the sin is not his; (2) it is a purely devotional command to increase his rank and to remind that his infallibility is a grace from God, not something earned. The author notes that the subtlety of attributing 'sin' to him here is similar to what was said in Surah Al-Fath, and considers this second interpretation closer to the truth.

Key Terms

عصمة
ذنب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves the Prophet committed a sin and needed forgiveness.
Correction: Sanusi shows the command is either for the community or a devotional act that actually confirms his sinlessness by emphasizing that his infallibility is a gift from God.

Key Takeaway

The Verse 'May Allah Pardon You' is Honor, Not Rebuke
Sanusi reinterprets a Quranic phrase as honorific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'May Allah pardon you' in Surah Al-Tawbah is interpreted as an honorific expression rather than a reproach.

Core Idea
Divine words to prophets are often honorific, not reprimands.

When Allah says to His Prophet 'May Allah pardon you,' some might mistakenly think it signals a fault. But Sanusi clarifies that this is a form of honor and respect, similar to how we address great people with polite phrases. This lesson unpacks that interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the contrast between 'reproach' and 'honor'.
The verse in context: after the Battle of Tabuk.
2
Identify the key claim
Sanusi explicitly states there is no reproach (معاتبة) at all. Instead, the phrase contains honor (تكرِمة) and veneration (تعظيم).
3
Understand the analogy
He compares it to saying 'May Allah rectify you and honor you' when beginning speech with dignitaries. This shows the phrase is a polite opening.
This analogy helps reframe the meaning.
4
Connect to a common misunderstanding
Many assume 'pardon' implies wrongdoing. Sanusi corrects this by showing it's a respectful formula.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا قولُهُ تعالى : عَفَا اللهُ عَنكَ ﴾ [التوبة : ٤٣] : فلا معاتبة فيهِ بوجه ، بل فيهِ تَكرِمةٌ وتعظيم ، كما يُقالُ في استفتاح الكلامِ معَ العظماء : أصلحَكَ اللهُ وأَعزَّكَ .
And as for His saying, the Exalted: 'May Allah pardon you' [Al-Tawbah 9:43]: There is no reproach in it at all. Rather, in it is honor and veneration, just as it is said at the beginning of speech with dignitaries: 'May Allah rectify you and honor you.'
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a brief explanation of the verse 'May Allah pardon you' (Surah Al-Tawbah). The author argues that there is no blame or reproach in it; instead, it is an expression of honor and respect. He compares it to saying 'May Allah rectify you and honor you' when addressing great people, showing that it is a form of politeness, not a rebuke.

Key Terms
معاتبة
تكرِمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The phrase 'May Allah pardon you' indicates the Prophet committed a sin or error.
Correction: Sanusi argues that it is purely an honorific, containing no reproach.

Key Takeaway

Verse on Spoils: No Blame on the Prophet
Sanusi explains that the verse refers to a previous decree, not a reprimand, and that the address is not to the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verse about spoils (Quran 8:68) refers to a prior decree permitting spoils, not a reprimand, and that the address in verse 8:67 does not include the Prophet or his elite companions.

Core Idea
The spoils verse honors the Prophet, not rebukes him.

This passage from Sanusi tackles a common misinterpretation of two verses from Surah Al-Anfal regarding the spoils of Badr. Many readers might think the verses imply that the Prophet and his Companions sinned by taking spoils before divine permission. Sanusi clarifies that the 'prior decree' mentioned is actually God's permission, making the verse a commendation of the Prophet's unique status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the two Quranic verses (8:68 and 8:67) that Sanusi explains.
Focus on the phrases 'كتاب سبق' (a prior decree) and 'معاتبة' (reproach).
2
Identify the two clarifications
Sanusi makes two main points: first, the 'prior decree' in verse 8:68 refers to God's permission of spoils for this ummah; second, the address in verse 8:67 is not directed at the Prophet or the elite companions.
3
Understand the reasoning
Notice how Sanusi connects verse 8:69 ('So eat from what you have gained as lawful and good') to support his interpretation. Also note the hadith about spoils being lawful only for Prophet Muhammad.
This shows the broader context of the verse.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding: that the verse implies sin. Sanusi directly refutes this by clarifying that it is about a prior decree and a unique favor.
This lesson guards against a wrong belief about the Prophet's infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ( لَوْلَا كِتَبٌ مِّنَ اللَّهِ سَبَقَ لَمَسَّكُمْ فِيمَا أَخَذْتُمْ عَذَابُ عظيم [الأنفال : ٦٨] : فالأظهرُ أنَّ معناه : لولا كتابٌ مِنَ اللهِ سبقَ بإحلال الغنائم لكم، وتخصيصكم بهذه الفضيلة دونَ مَنْ قبلكم .. لكانَ كذا وكذا، ولهذا قال تعالى : ﴿ فَكُلُوا مِمَّا غَنِمْتُمْ حَلَالًا طَيِّبًا [الأنفال : ٦٩]، فليس في الآيةِ إلزام ذنبٍ ولا معاتبة، بل فيها ذِكْرُ ما خُصَّ بهِ نبينا وسيدنا ومولانا محمَّدٌ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ وفُضْلَ بِهِ مِنْ بين سائر الأنبياء والرسل على جميعِهم الصلاةُ والسلام، فكأنَّهُ تعالى قال : ما كان هذا لنبي غيرِكَ، كما قال عليه الصلاةُ والسلام : أُحِلَّتْ لِيَ الْغَنَائِمُ وَلَمْ تَحِلَّ لِنَبِيُّ قَبْلِي. والخطاب بقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ تُرِيدُونَ عَرَضَ الدُّنْيَا ﴾ [الأنفال : ٦٧] إِنَّما هو لِمَنْ أرادَ مِنَ الناسِ ذلكَ وتَجرَّدَ غرضُهُ لعَرَضِ الدنيا وحدَهُ ، وللاستكثار منها ، وليس المراد به النبيَّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ولا علية أصحابه رضيَ الله تعالى عن جميعهم.
And as for His saying, the Exalted: 'If not for a decree from Allah that preceded, a great punishment would have touched you for what you took' [Al-Anfal 8:68]: The most apparent meaning is: If not for a decree from Allah that preceded by permitting spoils for you, and specifying you with this virtue over those before you... it would have been such and such. And for this reason He said: 'So eat from what you have gained as lawful and good' [Al-Anfal 8:69]. So there is no imputation of sin nor reproach in the verse; rather, in it is a mention of what our Prophet, our Master, our Mawla Muhammad (peace be upon him) was specified with, and the virtue he was given above all other prophets and messengers (peace be upon them all). It is as if He said: 'This was not for any prophet other than you,' just as he (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'Spoils were made lawful for me, and they were not made lawful for any prophet before me.' And the address in His saying: 'You desire the goods of this world' [Al-Anfal 8:67] is only for those among the people who desired that and whose sole purpose was the goods of this world and to amass them. The Prophet (peace be upon him) and the elite of his companions (may Allah be pleased with them all) are not intended.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of two related verses about the Battle of Badr. The author first addresses verse 8:68, which literally says 'if not for a prior decree, a great punishment would have touched you for what you took.' He explains that the 'prior decree' is God's permission of spoils for this community. Thus, the verse does not accuse the Prophet or the Companions of sin, but rather highlights the virtue of the Prophet in being permitted spoils, which no previous prophet had. The author then addresses verse 8:67, where the address 'You desire the goods of this world' is clarified to refer not to the Prophet or the leading Companions, but only to those whose sole aim was worldly gain. Thus, no blame attaches to the Prophet.

كتاب سبق
غنائم
معاتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 8:68 implies that the Prophet and Companions sinned by taking spoils before permission.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the 'prior decree' is God's permission, so the verse acknowledges a special favor, not a sin. He says: 'There is no imputation of sin nor reproach in the verse; rather, it is a mention of what our Prophet was specified with.'

Checkpoint

A) A decree of punishment for the disbelievers.
B) A decree permitting spoils for this Ummah.
C) A decree about the creation of the universe.
D) A decree about the prophethood of Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
The verse about spoils is not a reproach but a declaration of the Prophet's unique honor, as it refers to a prior divine decree permitting spoils for his community alone.

?

The Verse 'He Frowned and Turned Away' is Not a Sin
Sanusi quotes Qadi Iyad to argue that the frown was not a sin but a lesson for others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's frown in Surah Abasa was not a sin but an act of obedience.

Core Idea
The Prophet's frown was guidance, not guilt.

The opening verses of Surah Abasa describe the Prophet turning away from a blind man to speak to a wealthy disbeliever. Some have misunderstood this as a divine rebuke. Qadi Iyad clarifies that the verse does not indicate a sin; rather, it informs the disbeliever and shows that the Prophet's action was obedience to Allah. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the key terms: عبس, تولى, استئلاف.
2
Identify Qadi Iyad's main claim
Find the statement that directly says there is no sin. Understand that the verse is informing, not blaming.
The claim is: 'ليس فيه إثبات ذنب له عليه السلام'.
3
Understand the reasoning
The passage explains that the better course would have been to attend to the blind man, but the Prophet's actual action was still obedience—conveying the message and seeking to win over the disbeliever as Allah legislated.
This shows that the verse points out what was better, not that the Prophet sinned.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many think this verse rebukes the Prophet. Qadi Iyad corrects this: it is a lesson for others and a clarification of priorities, not a blame.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله جلَّ مِنْ قائل : عَبَسَ وَتَوَلَّى ...) [عبس: ١) الآية : فقال عياض « الشفا » : ليسَ فِيهِ إثباتُ ذنبٍ لهُ عليهِ السلامُ ، بل إعلامُ اللهِ ذلك المتصدى لهُ ممَّنْ لا يزَّكَّى ، وأنَّ الصواب والأولى كان لو كُشِفَ لك حال الرجلين لاخترت الإقبال على الأعمى ، وفعلُ النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ لِمَا فعل ، وتصدّيهِ لذلك الكافر .. كان طاعة الله ، وتبليغاً عنه ، واستئلافاً له كما شرعَهُ اللهُ تعالى له ، لا معصية ومخالفة لهُ.
And as for His saying, the Majestic: 'He frowned and turned away' [Abasa 80:1]—the verse—Qadi Iyad said in 'Al-Shifa': 'There is no establishing of a sin for him, peace be upon him, in it. Rather, it is Allah informing that person who was confronting him, who would not be purified, and that the correct and better course, had the situation of the two men been revealed to you, would have been that you would have chosen to turn toward the blind man. And the action of the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) in doing what he did and his turning to that disbeliever was obedience to Allah, conveying from Him, and seeking to win him over as Allah had legislated for him, not disobedience or opposition to Him.'
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the opening verses of Surah Abasa, where the Prophet frowned and turned away from a blind man to attend to a wealthy disbeliever. The author quotes Qadi Iyad from his work 'Al-Shifa' to argue that the verse does not prove any sin on the Prophet. Instead, it informs the person who was seeking guidance and would not purify himself. The better course was to attend to the blind man, but the Prophet's actual action (turning to the disbeliever) was still obedience to God—it was an act of conveying the message and seeking to win over the disbeliever as Allah had commanded. Thus, there was no disobedience.

تولى
استئلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'He frowned and turned away' is a rebuke and indicates that the Prophet committed a sin.
Correction: Qadi Iyad explains that the verse does not establish a sin; rather, it informs the disbeliever and shows that the Prophet's action was obedience, with the better course being attending to the blind man.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Read the passage and translation

بإحلال الغنائم لكم، وتخصيصكم بهذه الفضيلة دونَ مَنْ قبلكم .. لكانَ كذا وكذا ، ولهذا قال تعالى : ﴿ فَكُلُوا مِمَّا غَنِمْتُمْ حَلَالًا طَيِّبًا [الأنفال : ٦٩] ، فليس في الآيةِ إلزام ذنبٍ ولا معاتبة ، بل فيها ذِكْرُ ما خُصَّ بهِ نبينا وسيدنا ومولانا محمَّدٌ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ وفُضْلَ بِهِ مِنْ بين سائر الأنبياء والرسل على جميعِهم الصلاةُ والسلام ، فكأنَّهُ تعالى قال : ما كان هذا لنبي غيرِكَ ، كما قال عليه الصلاةُ والسلام : أُحِلَّتْ لِيَ الْغَنَائِمُ وَلَمْ تَحِلَّ لِنَبِيُّ قَبْلِي (۱)

والخطاب بقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ تُرِيدُونَ عَرَضَ الدُّنْيَا ﴾ [الأنفال : ٦٧] إِنَّما هو لِمَنْ أرادَ مِنَ الناسِ ذلكَ وتَجرَّدَ غرضُهُ لعَرَضِ الدنيا وحدَهُ ، وللاستكثار منها ، وليس المراد به النبيَّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ولا علية أصحابه رضيَ الله تعالى عن جميعهم
As for the making lawful of spoils for you, and singling you out with this virtue over those before you... it would be such and such. And therefore He says: 'So eat of what you have taken as spoils, lawful and good' (Al-Anfal 69). There is no imposition of sin or rebuke in the verse; rather, it is mention of what our Prophet and our Master Muhammad (peace be upon him) was singled out with and favored with over all other prophets and messengers (peace and blessings be upon them all). So it is as if He said: This was not for any prophet other than you, just as he (peace and prayer be upon him) said: 'Spoils were made lawful for me, and they were not made lawful for any prophet before me.' (1) And the address in His saying: 'You desire the goods of this world' (Al-Anfal 67) is only for whoever among the people desired that and whose aim was solely the goods of this world and seeking much of it; and the Prophet (peace be upon him) is not intended by it, nor the elite of his Companions (may God be pleased with them all).
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Prophet from the implication that he sinned by taking spoils. The author first explains that the verse permitting spoils is actually a special favor granted to Muhammad, not a rebuke. The phrase 'There is no imposition of sin or rebuke in the verse' directly counters the objection. The author then reinforces this by quoting the hadith that spoils were made lawful only for him. The second paragraph addresses the verse 'You desire the goods of this world,' clarifying that it is directed at those who sought worldly gain purely, and not at the Prophet or his leading Companions. The author thus preserves the impeccable righteousness of the Prophet and his elite followers.

Key Terms
الغنائم
الذنب
المعاتبة
الفضيلة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse rebukes the Prophet for taking spoils.
Correction: The author shows it is a grant of favor, not a rebuke.

Checkpoint

A) To rebuke the Prophet for taking spoils
B) To grant a special permission to the Prophet and his community
C) To forbid spoils
D) To compare the Prophet to previous prophets

Key Takeaway
The verse permitting spoils is a unique favor for Prophet Muhammad, not a rebuke.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Sanusi carefully clears the Prophet from any blame. How quickly do you jump to blame others when things go wrong? Are you willing to give them the benefit of the doubt, just as Sanusi does for the Prophet and his companions?

The Verse of 'Abasa Does Not Imply Sin
Sanusi presents two interpretations of 'Abasa to deny any sin by the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two classical interpretations of Surah 'Abasa that defend the Prophet from any implication of sin.

Core Idea
The verse of 'Abasa actually defends the Prophet, not blames him.

The verse of 'Abasa is sometimes misunderstood as rebuking the Prophet for frowning at a blind man. However, classical scholars like Qadi Iyad have provided clear explanations showing that the verse does not imply sin. This lesson presents two such interpretations: one from Qadi Iyad and another attributing the action to the disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its full literal translation. Note the key terms mentioned and the overall argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا إثبات ذنب' – no establishment of sin.
2
Analyze Qadi Iyad's argument
Identify how Qadi Iyad explains the verse: he states that it does not establish a sin but rather informs the Prophet of a better choice, and that the Prophet's action was actually obedience to God.
Notice the use of 'بل إعلام' – rather it is an informing.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
Read the second interpretation attributed to Abu Tammam, which suggests that 'he who frowned' refers to the disbeliever, not the Prophet. This completely removes any possible blame.
This shows that even the wording can be interpreted differently to avoid any implication of sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله جلَّ مِنْ قائل : عَبَسَ وَتَوَلَّى ...) [عبس: ١) الآية : فقال عياض الشفا » : ( ليسَ فِيهِ إثباتُ ذنبٍ لهُ عليهِ السلامُ ، بل إعلامُ اللهِ ذلك المتصدى لهُ ممَّنْ لا يزَّكَّى ، وأنَّ الصواب والأولى كان لو كُشِفَ لك حال الرجلين لاخترت الإقبال على الأعمى ، وفعلُ النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ لِمَا فعل ، وتصدّيهِ لذلك الكافر .. كان طاعة الله، وتبليغاً عنه، واستئلافاً له كما شرعَهُ اللهُ تعالى له، لا معصية ومخالفة لهُ

وما قَصَّهُ الله تعالى عليهِ مِنْ ذلكَ إعلام بحال الرجلين، وتوهين أمر الكافر عندَه، والإشارة إلى الإعراض عنه بقولِهِ : ﴿وَمَا عَلَيْكَ أَلَّا يَزَّكَّى ) (عبس: ٧) .

وقيل : أَرادَ بـ « عبس وتولَّى » : الكافر الذي كانَ معَ النبي صلَّى الله عليه وسلَّمَ، قاله أبو تمام ((۲)
As for His saying, May He be glorified: 'He frowned and turned away' (Abasa: 1) – the verse: Qadi Iyad said in al-Shifa: 'There is no establishment of sin for him (peace be upon him) in it. Rather, it is God informing him of the state of that which he addressed, who will not purify himself, and that the correct and better course was that had the case of the two men been disclosed to you, you would have chosen to face the blind man. And the action of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in what he did, and his addressing that disbeliever, was obedience to God, conveyance from Him, and winning him over as God had legislated for him; it was not disobedience or opposition to Him. And what God recounted to him of that is an informing of the state of the two men, and an undermining of the matter of the disbeliever in his view, and an indication to turn away from him by His saying: 'And what is upon you if he does not purify himself' (Abasa: 7). And it was said: He intended by 'He frowned and turned away' the disbeliever who was with the Prophet (peace be upon him). Abu Tammam said this.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents two defenses against the implication that the Prophet sinned when he frowned at the blind man. First, the author quotes Qadi Iyad's explanation that the verse does not establish a sin, but rather indicates that the better choice would have been to attend to the blind man. The Prophet's action was actually obedience, not sin. The second part of the verse is said to undermine the disbeliever. Then an alternative interpretation is given: that 'he who frowned' actually refers to the disbeliever, not the Prophet. This alternate view, attributed to Abu Tammam, completely removes any possible blame from the Prophet.

B) It shows the Prophet made a mistake

Key Takeaway
The verse of 'Abasa does not imply sin for the Prophet; it either informs of a better choice or refers to the disbeliever.

Adam's Disobedience: Linguistic vs Legal Sin
Sanusi distinguishes linguistic and legal sin in Adam's case and cites Ibn al-Arabi on tanzih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between linguistic and legal disobedience and how it defends prophetic infallibility in the case of Adam.

Core Idea
Adam's 'sin' was linguistic, not legal—a distinction that preserves his infallibility.

The Quranic verse 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and fell into error' (Taha: 121) may seem to imply that Adam committed a sin. However, scholars of kalam and tafsir have clarified that this refers to linguistic disobedience, not legal sin. In this lesson, we explore this critical distinction and its role in safeguarding the prophetic principle of 'isma (infallibility).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its English translation to get the author's complete argument.
Notice the incomplete quote at the end; we will discuss its significance.
2
Identify the two types of disobedience
Look for where the author defines 'linguistic disobedience' and 'legal disobedience'. Note the key differences.
Linguistic disobedience includes forgetfulness and misinterpretation; legal disobedience requires intentionality and knowledge of prohibition.
3
Understand the defended doctrinal point
Ask yourself: why does the author go to such lengths to argue that Adam's action was not legal disobedience? This safeguards the belief that prophets are infallible from deliberate sin.
4
Reflect on the incomplete citation
The passage begins a quote from Ibn al-Arabi on the necessity of declaring prophets transcendent (tanzih). Consider how this reinforces the argument.
The full quote is not provided here, but the point is clear: prophets must be respected above acts of disobedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَعَصَى عَادَمُ رَبَّهُ فَغَوَى ﴾ [طه : ۱۲۱] : فالتحقيق أنَّ المرادَ : المعصية والغوايةُ اللغويتانِ؛ وهما وقوع صورة المخالفة والغواية التي هي تركُ المراشد، سواء وقعا عمداً أو نسياناً أو تأويلاً، لا الشرعيتان؛ وهما المخالفة عمداً معَ العلم بالتحريم، فإنَّ المخالفة على هذه الصفة لم تقع مِنْ آدمَ عليه الصلاة والسلام، وإِنَّما وقعَتْ منه نسياناً أو بالتأويل، وذلك مبسوط في « الشفا » وكتب التفسير (۳)

ويرحم الله تعالى الإمام العالم ابن العربي حيث قال : ( يجب تنزيه
As for His saying: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and fell into error' (Taha: 121): the verified position is that what is intended is the linguistic disobedience and error, which are the occurrence of the form of contravention and error which is abandoning the right path, whether they occur intentionally, forgetfully, or by misinterpretation; not the legal disobedience and error, which are contravention intentionally with knowledge of prohibition. For contravention in this form did not occur from Adam (peace be upon him); rather it occurred from him forgetfully or by misinterpretation. This is elaborated in al-Shifa and the books of exegesis (3). And may God have mercy on the Imam, the scholar Ibn al-Arabi, where he said: 'It is necessary to declare transcendent...'
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defends the prophetic infallibility of Adam by distinguishing two types of disobedience: linguistic (لغوية) and legal (شرعية). The author asserts that the verse describes linguistic disobedience, not the legal one that involves intentional rebellion. Adam's action was not a deliberate sin but an error (نسيان) or misinterpretation (تأويل). The author cites other works for further detail. The passage concludes with an incomplete quote from Ibn al-Arabi on the necessity of declaring prophets transcendent (تنزيه).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Adam committed a deliberate sin deserving punishment, which would compromise his prophetic infallibility.
Correction: Adam's act was not a legal sin but a linguistic one—an error without moral blame, as it was done forgetfully or by misinterpretation.

B) Between linguistic and legal disobedience

Key Takeaway
The distinction between linguistic and legal disobedience preserves Adam's infallibility by showing his action was an error, not a deliberate sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own mistakes, do you often dismiss them as 'just a mistake' or 'I didn't mean it'? How does the distinction between linguistic and legal disobedience force you to examine the intention behind your actions? Commit to honestly naming your deliberate sins and repenting, rather than hiding behind forgetfulness.
?
The verse 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord' might have made you think less of Adam before this lesson. Now, knowing his action was not a sin, how does this change your understanding of God's mercy towards prophets—and towards you when you err unintentionally? Will you trust that God judges your heart's intent, not just the outward action?
?
Adam's 'error' was due to forgetfulness or misinterpretation. Are there areas in your life where you have been willfully ignorant or deliberately avoided seeking knowledge, excusing it as 'I didn't know'? What step will you take today to remove that ignorance, so that your actions align with truth?

Two Interpretations of 'He Frowned and Turned Away'
Two interpretations of the verse 'Abasa wa Tawalla'

Core Idea
The verse 'Abasa wa Tawalla' has two interpretations, one involving the disbeliever.

In this passage, the author discusses a key Qur'anic verse that is often misunderstood. He presents two interpretations: one derived from the context and another attributed to Abu Tammam. This helps clarify the proper understanding of the Prophet's conduct.

Adam's Disobedience: Linguistic vs. Legal
Sanusi clarifies the Qur'anic verse about Adam's disobedience using linguistic vs. legal distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between linguistic and legal disobedience and how it preserves prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Adam's 'disobedience' was a linguistic misstep, not a legal sin.

The Qur'an states that Adam disobeyed his Lord. Does this contradict the belief that prophets are infallible? In this passage, Sanusi clarifies that the term 'disobedience' used for Adam is linguistic, not legal. This distinction resolves the apparent conflict and upholds the prophetic stature of Adam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the terms 'linguistic' and 'legal'.
The passage begins by quoting the verse.
2
Identify the two categories
Find where Sanusi defines 'linguistic disobedience' and 'legal disobedience'. Note the conditions for each.
Linguistic: any act of opposition, even forgetfully. Legal: intentional with knowledge of prohibition.
3
Apply to Adam
Understand why Adam's action was only linguistic: it occurred forgetfully or by interpretation, not intentionally with full knowledge.
4
Reflect on implications
Ask yourself how this distinction protects the doctrine of prophetic infallibility (ismah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَعَصَى آدَمُ رَبَّهُ فَغَوَى﴾ : فالتحقيق أن المراد: المعصية والغواية اللغويتان؛ وهما وقوع صورة المخالفة والغواية التي هي ترك المراشد، سواء وقعا عمداً أو نسياناً أو تأويلاً، لا الشرعيتان؛ وهما المخالفة عمداً مع العلم بالتحريم، فإن المخالفة على هذه الصفة لم تقع من آدم عليه الصلاة والسلام، وإنما وقعت منه نسياناً أو بالتأويل، وذلك مبسوط في « الشفا » وكتب التفسير.
As for His statement, the Exalted: {And Adam disobeyed his Lord and erred} [Ta-Ha: 121]: The verification is that what is intended is the linguistic disobedience and error – they are the occurrence of the form of opposition and error which is leaving the right course, whether they occur intentionally, forgetfully, or by interpretation – not the legal [disobedience and error]; they are opposition intentionally with knowledge of prohibition. For opposition in this manner did not occur from Adam, peace be upon him. It only occurred from him forgetfully or by interpretation. And that is expounded in 'al-Shifa' and books of tafsir.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the verse 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and erred.' The author distinguishes between linguistic and legal meanings of 'disobedience' and 'error'. Linguistically, any act that goes against a command or misses the right path can be called disobedience or error, regardless of intent. Legally, disobedience requires intentional violation with knowledge that the act is prohibited. Adam's action was not legal disobedience because it occurred through forgetfulness or mistaken interpretation. Thus, his prophetic infallibility is maintained.

Key Terms
المعصية اللغوية
المعصية الشرعية
الغواية
آدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic statement 'Adam disobeyed his Lord' proves that Adam committed a sin, contradicting prophetic infallibility.
Correction: The disobedience attributed to Adam is linguistic, meaning any act of opposition irrespective of intent, not legal disobedience which requires intentional violation with knowledge of prohibition. Since Adam's act was forgetful or interpretive, it does not constitute a sin.

Checkpoint

A) Because he did not actually eat from the tree.

C) Because it was commanded by Allah.
D) Because it was a minor sin.

Key Takeaway
Adam's prophetic infallibility is preserved because his 'disobedience' was linguistic, not legal—it occurred forgetfully or by interpretation, not as intentional rebellion.

?
